FISH (EXPORT INSPECTION CHARGE) BILL 1981
Second Reading
Debate resumed from 7 May, on motion by !VII' Nixon:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
Mr SINCLAIR (New England-Leader of the House)-Mr Speaker, may I have your indulgence to suggest that the House have a general debate covering this Bill, the Fish (Export Inspection Charge) Collection Bill, the Dried Fruit (Export Inspection Charge) Bill and the Dried Fruit (Export Inspection Charge) Collection Bill as they are associated measures. Separate questions will, of course, be put on each of the Bills at the conclusion of the debate.
Mr SPEAKER-Is it the wish of the House that a general debate proceed? There being no objection I will allow that course to be followed.
Mr KERIN (Werriwa) (9.48)- Somehow the razor gang has brought the dried vine fruit industry and the fishing industry together at last. I do not know whether that has been an ambition of the Government for a long time but it has happened and we are discussing these two industries tonight in a cognate debate. Of course, what the Leader of the House (Mr Sinclair) said is truethere is a similarity because the legislation before us relates to charges. If I read the part of the razor gang's report which relates to these two industries honourable members will see the logic in the reasoning of the Leader of the House. The report states:
Wherever possible we intend to apply the 'user-pays' principle and introduce new or increased charges for Government services as the best possible determinant of the value of those services. Consistent with this we will be examining further the feasibility of requiring industry to meet the cost of membership of international commodity organisations. Charges will

also be introduced for actuarial and valuation services, archives services, the services provided to industry by the Australian Atomic Energy Commission, and for translation services and the assessment of overseas qualifications.
The program of export inspection charging, aimed at recovering approximately 50 per cent of total costs, is to he extended; in the case of fish and dried fruit from 1 July J981; of livestock, dairy products, eggs and honey from I July 1982; and of fresh, canned and processed fruit and vegetables from I January 1983.
So they are all going to cop it. The report further states:
A significant rationalisation of meat inspection services has already been commenced and will be finalised by the Australian Agricultural Council in August 1981.
That is very optimistic because 1 think the Minister for Primary Industry (Mr Nixon) has already pretty well put the kibosh on the whole dual meat inspection fiasco. As I say, the Minister is rather optimistic if he thinks he will rationalise that matter by the next Australian Agricultural Council meeting. These measures rel1ect the Government's hypocrisy on the question of export charges and there recoupment. When the Australian Labor Party was in governmcnt and tried to get back some of the costs of inspection imposed on the beef industry which was enjoying a boom in 1974, the then Opposition yelled loud and long. Of course, it pulled the charges off when it got into government and then reimposed them. There is a heck of a lot of hypocrisy in this business. We are becoming used to hypocrisy from this Government.
The purpose of the Dried Fruit (Export Inspection Charge) Bill is to impose charges for export inspection of dried fruit from I July 1981. It is the Government's intention to recovcr approximately half of total inspection costs for these products. A rate of $5.50 a tonne to be imposed on exports in 1981-82 is proposed and will be recommended to the Governor-General for promulgation in regulations. On the basis of long term export averages, charges at this rate will yield approximately $259,000 in a full year. By and large, the price being received for exports for dried vine fruits is about $1 ,000 or $1, I00 per tonne. Perhaps $5.50 is not an enormous amount of money to impose on the industry. I do not know why one of our weakest industries has been singled out to be the first to take on the new General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade policy for the levying of export charges. After going through considerable adjustment and reconstruction, this industry is still one of Australia's low income rural industries in terms of incomes available to farm families. By this, 1 refer to the net cash return for each family labour input-that is, a wage equivalent figure.

Fish (Export Inspection Charge) Bill

14 May 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2473

I will cite incomes for various regions. The figures are available due to the work of the Bureau of Agricultural Economics which published them in a recent quarterly review. For the Sunraysia district in New South Wales, the net cash return for a family labour unit was $7,923. There are about 446 growers in that area. In the mid-Murray district of New South Wales, the net cash return for the family unit was $4,761 and the number of growers about 50. For New South Wales as a whole, the nct cash return was only $7,489-in other words, it is below the average wage. Worse figures apply for Victoria and South Australia. For example, for Robinvale, Sunraysia in Victoria, the net cash return a family labour unit was $5,773. It is in this area that most of the industry is concentrated, there being 1,879 growers. For the mid-Murray area there was a net cash return of a mere $4,175 with 222 growers in the district. For Victoria as a whole, the average cash return is a little over $5,000. A similar low figure of $5,622 applies for the Riverland which has 1,076 growers. For all regions, the net cash return for a family labour unit is $6,042. That is for a total, within the BAE survey, of 3,773 growers.
This is an industry facing problems; it is a low income industry compared with the rest of the primary industries in terms of the way the Bureau of Agricultural Economics surveys. The horticultural industry is by far the lowest paying. It is facing problems in respect of reconstruction in the Riverland. The industry is facing problems with salinity along the Murray and in respect of decreasing yields. Essentially, dried vine fruit growers are price takers and not farmers who can influence the world market. It is very much a market in which there is a lot of competition. Export prices at the moment are slightly higher than in previous years due to major crop failures overseas. In the longer term, sustained recovery in the world DVF production could be expected to affect adversely real unit returns to Australian producers. Because, as [ have said, Australian DVF producers are essentially price takers on world markets-about 60 per cent of Australian production is exported-any increase in charges will affect the industry's ability to compete. Therefore, it is quite clear that the cost of the measure imposed by the Government will be passed back to the growers. Growers will have to pay the charge because of the pooling arrangment. The levy is expected to raise $260,000. If one does a mathematical division, one will see that each grower will forgo about $70 in income.
I will return to the basic point: Why begin this policy of cost recovery for inspection services as a

revenue raiser in one of the weakest rural industries? The industry has a lot of problems. By far the major problem is that of salinity. This Government is removing itself from all programs that relate to broader policies so essential to the health of Australian agriculture. We have already seen the Government try to get out of soil conservation and forestry. It would not surprise me if the Government does not try to get out water resources in this country. The equalisation scheme in the industry at present is not applicable due to the relative prices-export and domestic. Another stabilisation agreement between the industry and the Government as yet has not been agreed to. By and large, the present cost structure and the prices received overseas are such that stabilisation and equalisation are not immediate questions facing the industry with any urgency.
One of the industry leaders, Mr Gill, recently said that he would await the Government's approach in respect of the dairy industry in order to see what sort of direction the Government would take before he would enter into more serious negotiations with the Government. That is the only point the Opposition wishes to make. Having seen the vigour with which the Government is proceeding with most of its mad, crazy, razor gang recommendations, the Opposition believes there is not much sense in moving amendments. The Government will plough on regardless of what functions it is wiping out and the industries it will adversely affect.
The Fish (Export Inspection Charge) Bill is similar to the dried fruit Bill in that it intends to recoup part of the cost borne by the Commonwealth for inspecting fish for export. That is part of the Government's general program, as I have already said. The Bill is designed to ensure that products meet minimum standards of quality, hygiene and presentation when exported. In 1979 the Government introduced charges for meat, grain and wool inspection aimed at recovering approximately 50 per cent of inspection costs. The Government now proposes that fees aimed at a similar level of recovery should be imposed for similar services.
I will examine the fishing industry, particularly where the incidence of these charges will be borne most. The export sector of the industry largely is based on rock lobsters, prawns, oysters, pearl scallops and abalone. Fortunately, export markets for these products are lucrative. For example, the value of exports in 1978-79 for rock lobsters was $7Im. We are the world's largest producer and exporter of rock lobsters. Export sales of prawns
were worth $118m, scallops $5.lm and abalone $26m. Tuna is also exported but the markets are

2474 REPRESENTATIVES 14 May 1981

Fish (Export Inspection Charge) Bill

less certain. In recent years this industry has been characterised by fluctuating prices. Of course, this is the industry that recently received notice that the freeze on entry into the market for the southern blue fin tuna would be lifted. This move by the Government was clearly taken against the wishes of the rnajority of the industry and the processors, particularly Safcol. Suspicion is rampant in the industry that this move was taken simply to allow Heinz into the market. I cannot see any reason for that not being true. An enormous amount of disruption will be caused if a heap of people are allowed to come into the industry. The statement put to the Minister at that time was very poorly thought through. It is quite clear that the industry will face some problems in the near future.
The profitability of the domestic sector of the fishing industry in Australia is uncertain. For example, at this time Australian fishermen are concerned that the easy access granted to the domestic market for New Zealand fishermen is depressing prices. Under the New ZealandAustralia Free Trade Agreement and closer economic agreements, as well as talks under way at the moment, New Zealand fish enters Australia duty free.
The Australian Fishing Industry Council, in commenting on the Bill before the House, has suggested that if the user must pay-that is the direction of the Government-the user also should have some control over the way in which the inspection service is run. The Council regards the existing system as inefficient and costly and it claims that the system does not meet with the industry's requirements. No doubt it has brought that to the attention of the Minister but, in so many areas these days, the Minister does not seem to respond to the legitimate claims of industry. I think this goes along with the whole razor gang approach-the ideological approach to Australia's primary industries.
As I have said before, one can see and detect that the general direction of this Government's policies towards primary industry is more and more towards giving guarantees, backing up statutory marketing corporations and getting out of programs of assistance to specific commodity groups and to primary industry overall, and to try to settle the rest of the policy in terms of taxation deductions. I think there have been about 40 taxation deduction measures since this Government came to office, of which 12 or 13 specifically relate to primary industry. It seems that there will be more of these measures on the way because the Government already has given some notice of that. That might be a rational enough approach to

primary industry; certainly it is an accountant's approach and it accords to the Fraser ideology and the tenets of Fraserism, but it does avoid a lot of responsibility. It avoids the more sensible approach of looking at what the real needs of Australian agriculture are.
The Australian Labor Party believes that, in so many of these areas, we still need to identify the national problems. One of the big national problems we have at present is water quality. Water quality affects particularly the dried vine fruit growers of this country especially in the Sunraysia, Murray and Riverland regions of this country. I know there are amendments to the River Murray Commission Act before the Senate, but these will do nothing to deal with the prime question of water quality. The amendments will merely enhance the capacity of the River Murray Commission as an advisory body but will do nothing at all about water quality. Until all the governments involved with water quality in this country realise that they need to give up some of their parochial State powers, that they need to identify the real needs, hand over some powers and set up a jointly run regulatory body with powers to monitor water quality and not just quantity, we will not solve many of these problems. It is in areas such as water quality, soil conservation, quarantine, plague locusts and other general areas that the Government is abdicating its responsibility to the primary sector at present.
Mr TUCKEY (O'Connor) (10.3)-The purpose of the legislation before the House is to transfer to the export side of the fishing and the dried fruits industries part of the cost of supplying inspection services. This has already been done in the meat, grain and wool industries. As this transfer is one of relief to the Australian taxpayer, I have pleasure in rising to support the Bills. The Bills are not complicated but there are some matters of interest arising from them. Firstly, both the original Bills, the Fish (Export Inspection Charge) Bill and the Dried Fruit (Export Inspection Charge) Bill make the charges applicable to the exporter, even if that exporter is a State or State authority. This has significant administrative advantages in the collection of fees and the Government is to be commended for this decision. At this stage other industries subject to similar charges and levies are furiously debating who should pay for these services.
In the case of fish and dried fruit, the exporter is the processor. The factor that arises from this issue is that processors often argue that the benefit lies not with them as middlemen but with the producers. This argument is academic to say the least. All primary producers are well aware of where

Bills and are identical to each other in substance, meet the payment of these charges, we might ask

except for the products concerned. They require whether they can afford to do so. In Australian

payment of fees due within 28 days and section 6 terms, the charges to the dried fruit industry are

makes provision for a penalty equal to 10 per cent not large. In 1978-79 the Australian fish catch was

2476 REPRESENTATIVES 14 May 1981

Fish (Export Inspection Charge) Bill

63,395 tonnes. The value of fish products exported was $194.1m. The investment in boats and equipment was $247.5m. State governments also supply large amounts of infrastructure, such as harbours, jetties and shipways. These facilities are supplied at very low cost when compared with the rates and taxes paid by similar sized on-shore industries. It would therefore appear that the proposed inspection fees are very small by comparison.
It could further be asked: Why not levy the total charge on the industry and remove the entire burden from the taxpayer? I am committed to re lieving the taxpayer of all such charges. However, the question must come as to what is a reasonable charge. The Department of Primary Industry, which supplies the bulk of inspection services to primary export industries, employs 2,130 export inspection officers. Forty of them are fish export quality control officers. Practically all of the industries which are recipients of the export inspec tion services and which includes the largest userthe meat industry-are seasonal and cyclical. The industry itself accepts this in all its manning and award arrangements. Employment is often part time in order to fit in with the seasonal arrange ments. Pay scales, nevertheless, generally reflect the lack of permanency. Piece rates are common.
The Commonwealth, as the employer of the in spectors, seeks to ignore this fact. I am pleased to note that the Opposition is aware of this circum stance and that it may be suggesting, as I am, that the Commonwealth is not a suitable employer for these people. The Commonwealth employs in spectors as permanent public servants, offering them Public Service hours, conditions and holiday entitlements. The regular working hours of most of these inspection employees do not coincide with those of the process workers, nor do their public holidays. This creates additional cost to the processor above the charges levied upon him by fixed charges.
Another great problem in relation to the cost to the Government is the fact that, as all charges in these Bills are levied on a unit basis, drought years or bad fishing seasons greatly reduce production and consequently the fees collected from the pro cessor. However, as the employer, the Government must continue to carry its work force. The cost to the taxpayer and the deleterious effect to the workers, who must attend although there is no work available, are both bad. There is little need for the Commonwealth to employ these inspec tors in the manner that is currently in existence. To avoid the seasonal and cyclical problems that I
have mentioned the inspectors are almost per petually in living away from home circumstances

as they move around to follow the fish, the fruit or the abattoir kill. The cost of travelling expenses for meat inspectors alone in 1979-80 was $3.8m. The inspectors have established a strong union and lobby. They are aware that they can stop these delicately balanced perishable industries by removing their services for even a few days. Therefore, they can demand rates of pay and con ditions beyond the requirements of their trade.
This brings me to the real thrust of my speech. Although it is quite right for the Government to ensure that a high standard of product leaves our country-in fact, some countries will not approve imports without such government to government assurances-this does not relieve the Government of the responsibility to ensure that we supply the most efficient inspection service and at the lowest cost. Whilst the Government has the total responsibility for meeting the cost, there is some argument that economies will be considered. The Kelly report on meat inspection certainly does not support this argument. When the Government has total control of supplying the service yet can call on others to pay part or all of the cost, it is obvious that very little control will occur. The sacred cows of health regulation and safety are magnificently suited to building large government bureaucracies, insulated with the supporting argument that the cost is of no importance when com pared with the need. It is of considerable import ance if one is paying the. bill and can watch inefficiencies and overstaffing occurring every day.
The government seeks payment of the services it offers from the meat industry and the airline industry. Now we seek contributions from the fishing and dried fruits industries. In each case we allow no input on the cost or efficiency of our ser vice from those who are paying for it. A number of my constituents have questioned the need for excellence in terms of the manpower supplied and the cost in terms of efficiency of these services. If, as this legislation currently provides, we require arm's-length industry to pay for the service, we must give it an input about how muc h it should cost. We must look closely at the efficiency of our operation. In many cases total reconstruction is necessary to acknowledge the seasonal and cycli cal nature of these industries. Part time workers, sub-contractors and direct employment might be the answer, provided in each case the Federal input remains at the supervisory level. r call on the Government to consider seriously our position in this matter. l have great pleasure in supporting the Bills.
Mr CUNNINGHAM (M cMil lan)
(1 0.17)-As has been stated by the honourable

Fish (Export Inspection Charge) Bill

14 May 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2477

member for Werriwa (Mr Kerin), the opposition does not oppose these Bills. In the short time left this evening I wish to make a few comments about the Bills. The uproar in the House earlier made it rather difficult for me to say a few words about wool. In the few minutes available to me this evening perhaps I can cover a few of the matters relating to the agricultural scene covered by these Bills and a few of the issues raised by the honourable member for O'Connor (Mr Tuckey).
The first point I wish to make is that it is strange that the Government has decided at this time to take some $500,000 from the fishing industry and some $259,000 from the dried fruit industry in the manner decided. At this time, as we have been told many times by members of the National Country Party, most rural industries are facing larger increases than would be expected to be seen in the consumer price index figures, particularly in relation to fuel costs. The fisherman would know all about that. The high cost of fuel has really hurt those industries. Even though in some cases export prices for the commodities of fishermen are high, many of them, particularly the ones in my electorate, are not doing terribly well. They would be placed in a very difficult position if the task of finding the $500,000 for their industry were passed down to them. As the honourable member for O'Connor has said, the exporter is the one who is expected to pay but in most of these cases the person at the bottom of the rung who cannot pass on the costs will usually bear the brunt of any charge that is imposed on an industry such as this.
As the honourable member for O'Connor mentioned, the exporter pays principle would apply to a State authority. It appears that the razor gang's approach to these matters is that State governments and anything to do with State governments are open slather. The Government preaches small government and less taxes, but it acts hastily to raise as much money as it can and it does not appear to mind where it takes it from, even if it means taking it from a State authority and imposing more and more costs on the States. No doubt the honourable member for O'Connor would be in support of that approach. If this approach continues, perhaps the Government's federalism policies will force State governments to impose income taxes. That has been the whole process of the razor gang. That is what it has been all about.
Some of the smaller or weaker industries have decided that they cannot really afford to have this sort of money taken out to bear the brunt of that action. But then again, because the exporters are paying, we see in that clause of the Bill dealing

with penalties for late payment---which was mentioned by the honourable member for O'Connor---that the figure is set at 10 per cent. No doubt any shrewd businessman faced with the present situation, where we virtually have a credit squeeze, with interest rates going up non-stop, would be very happy to let the Government waive that 10 per cent. it is probable that businessmen are paying 15 per cent and 18 pcr cent for the money that they are trying to borrow.
There is not much doubt that this Bill will be taking funds oll those who are earning their living actually catching the fish. They will pay the bill but those who will get the cheap interest rate will be the exporters who possibly will be able to pass the costs right down the line. The honourable member for O'Connor took the usual approach that he takes in most of the speeches he has made in this House and he had a go at the Public Service. It is very rarely that we have seen him rise to speak-in the few speeches he has made to date in the House-- where he has not had a crack at the Public Service. He seems to take the view that the person who happens to work for the Government is inefficient and cannot do anything. I do not know from where he gets his facts. In debate after debate 1have been waiting to hear some facts concerning the razor gang which will prove that public servants have some queer approach, that they are not as efficient as those employed in industry and that they cannot do the job of inspection as well as anybody in private enterprise.
it appears that this Government is definitely taking a line, regardless of where it is, to raise funds. This is just another method that it is using. We do not object to the fact that industry should pay a certain amount of inspection costs because this is something in which the Labor Party believes. For too long the Government has been fostering many very well-off rural industries and using public funds to support inspections and the like whereas the industries are able to do it themselves. In this case as part of the razor gang review approach, this Government has decided that here is another way to get a crack at the States and since this opportunity has presented itself it is one way of doing it. We all know that the $259,000 allocated to the dried fruits section is not a great amount of money but it will affect industry. At present any money taken out of industry is a further charge on that industry. It is more money going into the Government coffers. I repeat that it is another method of swallowing the funds of the Government so that the industry pays inspection charges and the Government saves in its Budget allocation. As time is short I seek leave to continue my remarks at a later time.

ADJOURNMENT Glebe Estate-Drought in New South Wales-Parliament: Accommodation-Radio Australia-Electoral Redistribution in Western Australia-Proposed Sale of Belconnen Mall-Domestic Communications Satellites Motion (by Mr Street) propo~ed:
That the House do now adjourn.
Mr UREN (Reid) (10.24)-Tonight I want to raise the question of the actions of the razor gang in selling the Glebe Estate. I want to deal with the insecurity and fear that this decision has brought to those people living on that estate. In my view this was a violent, crude and brutal action on the part of the Fraser Government. Some 750 families are living in this area. They are very insecure and do not know what their future is, both in the short and long term. The decision which was taken back in 1974 to acquire the Glebe Estate was very progressive. At that time. I was a Minister in the Whitlam Labor Government which acquired the Glebe Estate for $17.sm. The estate was 47 acres and on it there were 750 dwellings. We bought the estate for three reasons: Firstly, to protect the people living on that estate; secondly, to protect the townscape which had been there for over 100 years; and, thirdly, to prevent the western freeway planned by the New South Wales conservative government from cutting a broad swathe through that estate. Once the estate became the property of the Australian Government we gave notice to the New South Wales Government that it could not develop a freeway through the estate which would have destroyed it to a great extent.
By acquiring the 750 properties on the Glebe Estate the Government intervened in the public housing sector. People on low incomes living in Paddington and most of the inner suburbs of Sydney, and in the inner suburbs of Melbourne, such as Carlton and Fitzroy, were forced to the fringes of those cities. The Government, by intervening, was able to allow those people on low incomes to

remain in the inner areas of those cities. Therefore, it was decided that a program of rehabilitation would be carried out over a period of five years at a cost of $lOm. Unfortunately the present Government has delayed that rehabilitation program. It has rehabilitated only about one seventh of the properties involved over a period of some six to seven years. Frankly, this rehabilitation will cost a lot more. But one has to recognise that this is an investment. It cost us $17.5m. We acquired the estate in a single purchase from the Church of England. The fact is that the value of the properties on that estate is between $40m and $70m. The value of the terrace dwelli'ngs in that area has grown to ~uch an extent that they cost about $100,000.
At the same time we were able to protect those people living in Wolloomooloo which was a similar development. Under those circumstances we made available $17m to the then Lewis conservative gov~rnment and we made available $12m worth of land. We gave this to the conservative government of New South Wales in order to protect the people of Wolloomooloo. It seems to me that in this situation similar action could be taken and the Commonwealth land could be transferred to the Government of New South Wales. I warn honourable members that the action carried out by the Whitlam Labor Government will be protected by the Federal Labor Party, by the State Labor Government, by local government authorities and by the trade union movement. It will be protected by the National Trust, the Glebe Society and the people in that area. We will not allow this land to be sold to the private sector. My colleague the honourable member for Sydney (Mr Les McMahon) and I will be at a public meeting on Sunday to defend the rights of these people.
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr MilIar)- Order! The honourable member's time has expired.
Mr O'KEEFE (Paterson) (10.29)-Once again attention must be drawn to the devastating drought which exists in the country areas of New South Wales. No rain has fallen since February and the Glenbawn Dam on the Hunter River is down to below 8 per cent of its capacity. The Keepit Dam on the great Namoi River is down to below 8 per cent of its capacity and there is just a trickle of water in the Chaffey Dam east of Tamworth. The situation for primary producers in those areas is very serious. Wyangala Dam and Burrendong Dam are in similar condition. Going into winter without water and feed is very serious, not only for primary producers but also for all the people in New South Wales and Australia. Stock numbers are down very considerably and there

Adjournment

14 May 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2479

has been a loss of crops. Many farmers in the wheat area of New South Wales have not had a crop of wheat for the past three seasons. As honourable members know, if we do not get rain in May, June and July there will be no wheat this year. This is serious for the farmers and country towns surronding the wheat areas.
It is also serious for the railways for they look to the wheat crop to bring in revenue & We had a very poor crop of wheat last year. It was one of the lowest on record. Without a good crop of wheat the railways in New South Wales will suffer a very serious loss of revenue. Small businesses in the country towns are affected because they rely on the prosperity of the people in the rural areas around them. It is evident that this Government will have to assist the New South Wales Government in the provision of funds to these drought affected areas. Of course the conditions that apply to assistance are such that when the State Government has spent $1 Om in drought relief the Federal Government provides $3 for every dollar provided by the State. It is up to the New South Wales Premier and his government to apply to the Federal authorities for drought relief funds. The Minister for Primary Industry (Mr Nixon) has stated that it is difficult on many occasions under the existing conditions for the New South Wales Government to apply for funds which are available.
Our national export income will be reduced. We rely on the production of wheat, wool and meat for our export income. With drought conditions the loss of production of these commodities will seriously affect the export income not only of New South Wales but also of Australia. I do not need to tell the House that the abattoirs in New South Wales are having great problems because of the stock shortages. Many of them have closed. Many of the local government abattoirs are calling on the New South Wales Government for financial assistance, I understand to the extent of $56m. No doubt we are over-serviced by abattoirs but, the conditions that have been brought about by a shortage of stock have exacerbated the problems that the abattoirs are facing at present.
Service industries in the country towns, such as machinery distributors, fuel distributors and motor car and lorry distributors, wilJ all be affected seriously with the result that unemployment will be rife. I can only emphasise to the people of Australia the problems we face in New South Wales. Local government bodies which provide services for people in country areas will experience financial difficulties in the collection of rates because if the people in those areas are

aiTected economically they will have difficulty in paying their rates.
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr Millar)- Order! The honourable member's time has expired.
Mrs CHILD (Henty) (10.34)-1 draw the attention of the House tonight to a situation of concern to myself and other honourable members. I refer to the question of parliamentary accommodation and the Parliament's right to control its own administration. All honourable members are well aware of the problems we face in this building and of the fact that these problems will continue until the new and permanent Parliament House is compeleted and open for business. During the last Parliament the Presiding Officers circulated to all members and senators a document entitled 'Accommodation for the Parliament', but there were several problems with that document.
First, it was circulated towards the end of the 1980 autumn session when there was little time for adequate consideration and discussion of its contents. Second, despite the fact that the pennanent heads of all five parliamentary departments were asked to supply options for the removal of staff from this building to other locations, the only options furnished were those relating to the Parliamentary Library. Third, despite the recognition in Appendix C of the Library submission of the need for Library staff to have face-to-face contact with members and senators, often at very short notice, in order to provide information sought, this appears to have been ignored or glossed over by the Presiding Officers. There are further problems which time will not allow me to elaborate on at present.
During the last Parliament and again in this Parliament, the leader of the Opposition (Mr Hayden) gave notice of two motions under General Business, which appear as Nos 8 and \0 on the Notice Paper and which relate to the Parliamentary Library and the Parliament itself respectively. Both of those notices indicate a high level of concern on the part of the Opposition about the functioning of the Parliament and a vital and integral part of the parliamentary process, that is, the Parliamentary Library. If the Government were really serious about the parliamentary process it would bring those notices on for debate at an early date. Indeed, it could already have done so earlier in this session when there was so little legislation before the Parliament. I note in passing that the Senate has also established a select committee on the Parliament's

2480 REPRESENTATIVES '14 May 1981

Adjournment

appropriations and staffing and that it has not yet reported.
In the last few years, and especially since 1975, there has been much talk of parliamentary reform. However, against this background of demonstrated concern about the effectiveness of Parliament we now have further indications of reduced services and reduced effectiveness. 1 refer to the recent article in the National Times headed 'Black Ban on Library Move'. When Library staff are forced to contemplate industrial action to preserve the level of service to all members and senators, it is a black day for this Parliament. The staff members are, by and large, professionals who have provided a high level of service, under difficult conditions, to members and senators from all parties.
On 19 February 1980 my colleague the honourable member for Melbourne Ports (Mr Holding) was contacted by the Professional Officers' Association with regard to this matter. He replied on the following day. I seek leave to have those letters incorporated in Hansard, together with a letter from the Labor Caucus to the Presiding Officers.
Leave granted.

PROFESSIONAL OFFICERS' ASSOCIATION
Australian Public Service
Suite 3, 46-48 Colbie Court, (P.O. Box 444) Woden. ACT 2606 Phone: (062) 824030 19 February 1980
Dear Mr Holding, I refer to proposals for the movement of certain client ser-
vice staff of the Parliamentary Library from Parliament House to a more distant location at the Hotel Kurrajong.
You will recall that, in November 1979, the Professional Officers' Association sent you a copy of a submission concerning this proposed made to the Presiding Officers by the Combined Library Unions Committee (CLUC). which represents all unionists in the Parliamentary Library.
That submission pointed out that the proposed movement of Library client service staff would have a most adverse effect on the standards of service the Library could provide to Senators and Members.
On behalf of those involved, who are concerned to maintain the standards of service offered by the Library, I seek your support in having excluded from the proposed relocations those Library Staff members who provide a direct client service.
Yours sincerely. Mary Wilson
Federal Industrial Officer, POA and on behalf of Kevin Cains

Ms Mary Wilson Federal Industrial Officer Professional Officers Association PO Box 444

Dear Ms Wilson,
Thank you for your letter of 19 February 1980, regarding the proposed movement of certain client service staff of the Parliamentary Library to the Hotel Kurrajong.

Let me state, at the outset, my grave concern over these proposals. If the Parliament is to function at aU, then all Members and Senators must have access to information that is
as complete as possible at the time it is required, is relevant and accurate, is speedily available and (sometimes) high ly confidential. I believe that this definition would be supported by all Members and Senators regardless of their Party affiliations.

I am in strong support of the submission made to the Presiding Officers of the Parliament by the Combined Library Unions Committee in November 1979. I do nol believe that the increased cost of the proposed move or the decline in ser-
vices that would result can be justified in any way.
Along with every other Member and Senator, r am well
aware of the accommodation problems in the present Parliament House. and the need for more space for such purposes as Committee Rooms, However, I believe that the most effective and least expensive resolution of this problem would be the erection of two demountable buildings at the rear of the building. These demountablcs would not be additions to the building in the normal sense, being freestanding. My proposal is that they be erected over the delivery road at the level of the middle floor of the existing building.

The new rooms thus created would be easily accessible to
all Members and Senators and, most importantly, would provide direct access to the Members Dining Room for the supply of meals to any Committee meetings during lunch or dinner
breaks.

Please convey my support to all members of the Parliamentary Library staff and my appreciation of their services to me
in the past.

I am enclosing copies of two articles I hope will be useful.
The example of the Congressional Research Service's increase in the number and range of services available is one which I hope the Parliamentary Library will emulate, where
appropriate.

Yours sincerely,

CLYDEHOLDING,MP

Adjournment

want to make in this House is this: It is difficult enough for the Indonesians to accept that a government owned and financed broadcasting network is not subject to government endorsement of everything that is broadcast; we only compound their suspicions by making formal representations at a diplomatic level. If we want to tell the Indonesians that the Government cannot control Radio Australia, we must let Radio Australia sort out its own relationships with Indonesian authorities. I believe that in that respect there are certain matters that Radio Australia has to attend to.
There have been reports of certain inaccuracies in some of the broadcasts that Radio Australia has made. For example, I spoke to one leading dissident in Jakarta, a critic of the Government who, by the way, enjoyed listening to Radio Australia; so I do not say that he is a critic of Radio Australia. I asked him whether he was aware 'of any inaccuracies that had been broadcast over Radio Australia news broadcasts. He said: 'Yes, indeed'. There was one inaccuracy that concerned him. A Radio Australia broadcast had said at one stage that he had been arrested by government authorities. When he went along to a social function in Jakarta that night, he was ribbed by people who thought that he was supposed to have been in gaol because they had heard on Radio Australia that he had been arrested. These incidents may be few and far between but they have to be corrected if they have arisen. I just put to the House that we really ought to allow the Indonesian Government and Radio Australia-in particular, I suppose the ABC-to work out their own relationships.
We say that the ABC is independent. That is the basis of the argument that we put to the Indonesian Government when we are faced with representations about the supposed shortcomings of Radio Australia. Ifthat is our view, we ought to say that the ABC should sort out its relationships with the Indonesian Government. I think that Australian journalists who urge that we should get tough with Indonesia-to use their words-on such matters at a government to government level are defeating their own purposes. The more we make representations at a government level, the more we convince the Indonesians that we control Radio Australia.

The first mechanism it has used is to extend the metropolitan area to include various portions which were previously in country electorates. We have to remember that in Western Australia there are more than twice as many members in city electorates as there are in country electorates. Parliament itself and not by any electoral COllmission. The Parliament sits in determination of the boundaries of those seats as proposed by the government of the day. What the government of the day has done on this occasion is to rearrange the boundaries of those four seats in an attempt to ensure that the Liberal Party continues to hold three of them. It has made the Kimberley seat, which in any event was one of the smaller country seats, into one of the largest country seats by any judgment to ensure that none of the other statutory seats fall to Labor.
The Pilbara seat was as large as any metropolitan seat; yet instead of reorganising it to ensure that the people of the Pilbara have sensible representation the Government has manipulated the boundaries to ensure that the Liberal Party will hang on to the Pilbara and the Labor Party will hang on to the Kimberley with a vastly increased enrolment. Yet at the same time it has done nothing about increasing the enrolments of the other two statutory seats, which enrolments are vastly smaller. Mr BARRY JONES (Lalor) (10.57)-1 raise a very important question of public policy relating to communication satellites. At 10.20 this evening, after I had received some information and checked the details, I telephoned the office of the Minister for Communications (Mr Sinclair) so that he would have due warning and would be in a position to make an appropriate reply or to give an undertaking to investigate the matter and report back. An Australian firm has lodged a $5m deposit with the RCA corporation in the United States for the purchase of a domestic satellite with 23 channels up and 23 channels down and one channel dedicated to microwave transmission. It is one of the new technology satellites which will be located further out in space than the Intelsat satellites which are already in orbit. Being further out, the RCA satellite will be able to cover the whole of the Pacific basin including Australia, New Guinea, New Zealand and possibly even the east coast of Africa. (Quorum formed). 14 May 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2487 ANSWERS TO QUESTIONS

The following answers to questions were circulated:

Flying Training Schools
(Question No. 980)
Mr Morris asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 1 April 1981:
(I) How many flying training schools are there in Australia, where are they located and under what name do they operate.
(2) What requirements does his Department lay down for establishing a flying training school.
(3) What checks does his Department make of the standard of flying and ground instruction at each flying training school.
(4) How many persons currently hold (a) student pilafs licence, (b) restricted private pilot's licence and (c) private pilot's licence.
(5) How many hours flying are required as a minimum to obtain a (a) restricted private pilot's licence and (b) private pilot's licence.
(6) What average number of hours are being logged to obtain each of these licences.
(7) What proportion of persons obtaining a student pilot's licence have obtained a (a) restricted pilot's licence and (b) private pilot's licence in each of the last 5 years.
(8) How many aircraft accidents have there been in Australia in each of the last 5 financial years involving (a) student pilots, (b) restricted private pilots and (c) private pilots.

(2) My Department lays down requirements for establishing flying schools in Air Navigation Orders Part 80, Section 80.1.
(3) My Department makes the following checks of flying and ground instruction at flying training schools:
(a) Annual surveillance of each school for licence renewal. This includes checks of flying training records, training syllabuses, students' progress records in theory subjects and the Chief Flying Instructor's record of flights with his staff for their annual checks.
(b) Biennial flight and ground checks of each flight instructor.
(c) Where a school employs a person who is approved to conduct licence or rating tests, up to 20 per cent of candidates prepared by the school are tested by a Departmental examiner. In other cases all tests are conducted by a Departmental examiner.
(d) Approval interviews and flight testing of all flying instructors wishing to be approved to conduct licence tests.
(e) Written examinations covering essential aeronautical knowledge and the principles and methods of instruction for all instructor rating candidates.
(f) Approval and observation of flight instructor refresher courses.
(4) As at 31 December 1980 there were: (a) 20,471 student pilot licence holders; (b) 6943 private pilot licence holders with area restriction;
and (c) 16,922 private pilot licence holders without area
restriction.
(5) The minimum number of hours required to obtain a private pilot licence with area restriction is 33 hours. The minimum number of hours required for the lifting of the area restriction on a private pilot licence is 22 hours.
(6) No comprehensive records of actual hours taken to achieve issue of flight crew licences are kept by my Department. An estimate of the average number of hours logged to obtain a private pilot licence with an area restriction is 45 hours. An estimate of the average number of hours logged to obtain a private pilot licence without an area restriction is 68 hours.

Office of Road Safety
(Question No. 981)
Mr Morris asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 1 April 1981:
(l) Did he say in answer to my question No 164 (Hansard 4 December 1980, page 470) that a resource limitation was that an adequate number of suitably qualified staff was not available to launch and monitor research and promotion projects of the Office of Road Safety.
(2) Was the limitation on qualified staff during 1980 due to the loss of qualified and experienced officers caused by resignation and/or transfer to other departments emanating from the compulsory transfer of the Office of Road Safety from Melbourne to Canberra; if not, what was the specific nature of the resource limitation referred to in his answer to question No. 164.
Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) Yes.
(2) The limitation on staff during the period referred to in my answer to question No. 164-i.e. during the twelve months to 30.6.80-was due to the loss of qualified and experienced officers as a result of resignation and/or transfer to other areas of Department of Transport Australia and to other organisations coupled with the inevitable delay in replacing such highly specialised officers. I am unable to say why the officers resigning or transferring chose to seek other positions.

Northern Airlines
(Question No. 1039)
Mr Morris asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 2 April 1981:
(1) Did Northern Airlines recently withdraw from aviation activities in the Northern Territory; if so, is he able to say for what periods and in respect of which sections of coastline were coastal surveillance activities for which Northern Airlines had responsibility not carried out.
(2) What specific arrangements were made for performance of relevant coastal surveillance duties not carried out by Northern Airlines during the periods, and at the locations concerned.
(3) What specific arrangements have been made for performance of the coastal surveillance duties previously covered by contracts with Northern Airlines and what are the precise financial provisions in each case.
Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(l) Northern Airlines ceased operations in the Northern Territory on 6 February 1981. The company, in a managerial role only, continued to provide surveillance coverage through sub-contractors to allow time for a new contract to be let. While this was being arranged, ad-hoc contractors were called in at short notice. Coverage was not able to be provided as follows:
Littoral Search of Bathurst and Melville IslandsFebruary 7 to 9
Littoral Search from Darwin to Millingimbi-February 7t09.
Offshore Search of refugee approach routes-February 7 to 14.
(2) Ad-hoc coverage was provided by Arnhem Air Charter. Nomad aircraft operated on behalf of the Customs Bureau were on stand-by.
(3) The surveillance duties formerly carried out by Northern Airlines are now being performed by Ansett to whom the Northern Airlines surveillance contracts have been assigned. The financial provisions of the contracts remain unchanged.

North West Shelf Gas and Petroleum Project
(Question No. 1085)
Mr Morris asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 7 April 1981:
(1) What is the estimated sum of public funds required for (a) port infrastructure and (b) road and rail infrastructure for the North West Shelf gas and petroleum project.
(2) What is the division of costs and responsibilities between Commonwealth and State Governments in the development and operation of the port.

(3) To what degree will the port facilities be owned or leased or operated by the private companies owning the minerals to be exported.
(4) Is there any requirement for the mineral exports to be transported in Australian flag ships, in line with the Government's foreign investment guidelines on mineral ownership.
Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) The provision of port infrastructure and road/rail infrastructure within the development area in the vicinity of Dampier, and in the Burrup Peninsula, is a matter for the Western Australian Government. I understand that the State Government is contributing (under the agreement with the Joint Venturers) $1 million to the infrastructure costs of roads on the Burrup Peninsula to service this development. Local Government is also likely to contribute funds towards road infrastructure for the project. The total requirement for public funds is not known.
(2) Provision of portfacili ties for the North West Shelf Gas Development project is primarily the responsibility of the Western Australian Government. The Commonwealth has no direct responsibility for the development and operation of the port nor for meeting costs.
(3) I understand the supply base and export facilities at the port will be owned and operated by the Joint Venturers. The ownership and operation of a materials handling facility, to be constructed for the development project by the Joint Venturers, may revert to the proposed Port Authority on completion of the construction phase. The State Government is discussing legislation for the Port Authority with the Joint Venturers.
(4) There is no requirement for the mineral exports to be transported in Australian flag ships. The Government supports the use of Australian flag ships in the overseas trades where their operation is commercially viable, and where additional costs of such transportation are not imposed on the industry or the community.
Sky West Airlilles: Ban On Smoking In Aircraft
(Question No.1087)
Mr Morris asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 7 April 1981:
(1) Has his attention been drawn to the recenl news reports that a Western Australian commuter operator, Sky West Airlines, has banned smoking in its aircraft.
(2) Has he had or will he have discussions with other Australian airlines for extension of the ban on smoking in aircraft especially on short haul routes.
Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) Yes, I commend Sky West Airlines for their initiative in this matter.
(2) While I am sympathetic to the problems faced by nonsmokers on aircraft, it is extremely doubtful whether the Commonwealth has any authority to ban smoking on aircraft except in matters relating to safety, such as during take-off, landing, refuelling, and in toilet areas. Although banning of smoking in aircraft in addition to the above restrictions is a matter for each individual operator, I do consider that all passenger air service operators should reflect community attitudes and expectations. I have and will continue to encourage the airlines to keep this matter under review to ensure that any alteration in the demand for non-smoking seats is rel1ected in their decisions.

Answers to Questions

14 May 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2493

Trans~Australia Airlines
(Question No. 1163)
Mr Morris asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 8 April 1981 :
(1) Has his attention been drawn to a report on page 2 of the Australian Financial Review of 3 April 1981 where he is reported to have told the Chairman of the Australian National Airlines Commission that the Government would hold all shares if TAA was formed into a public company.
(2) Is there any substance to this report; if so, when did he advise the Chairman of the Commission that TAA was to be formed into a public company.
Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) and (2) I am aware of the Press report referred to by the honourable member.
I spoke to the Chairman of the Australian National Airlines Commission on Tuesday, 31 March 1981 to inform him that the Government had resolved in principle to establish TAA as
a public company. r informed him that the structure of the
company had yet to be determined but that I thought that the Government would establish it as a company similar to Qantas. I rang him again on Wednesday 1 April saying that the Government had asked for a paper setting out a number of options that it could consider in establishing TAA as a public company.
Might I say that all the options that have been canvassed so far are purely hypothetical. The simple fact is that when I spoke to the Chairman of the Australian National Airlines Commission the Government had not considered that issue.

Home Telephone Rentals: Department of Transport
(Question No. 1532)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
(1) How many officers and employees of his Department have their home telephone rentals and/or charges (a) fully or (b) partially paid by his Department.
(2) What was the cost to his Department of this expenditure in 1979-80.
Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) (a) and (b) Where an official telephone service has been installed at the private residence of an officer Finance Direction No. 13/12 of the Department of Finance authorises the Department concerned to make payment of the full basic rental plus up to 120 metered calls per half-year plus official trunk calls. Put simply, all of the basic rental and part of the telephone calls are paid for by the Department. Nine hundred and sixty one key operational, policy, technical and administrative staff in the Department of Transport have official telephones at their private residences in accordance' with these provisions.
(2) $131,662.

Re~cycling ofPaper: Department of the Prime Minister and Cabinet
(Question No. 1570)
Mr Bungey asked the Prime Minister, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:

Coastal Surveillance
(Question No. 1681)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
(1) How many (a) vessels and (b) aircraft were engaged in patrolling the north-west coast of Western Australia during 1979-80.
(2) How many foreign fishing vessels were reported or sighted fishing illegally in the area and what action was taken.

(3) From which country did each reported or sighted fishing vessel come.
Mr Hunt-Th~ answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) (a) An average o( two RAN patrol boats per month supplemented for short periods by charter vessels and one 'Cape' Class navigational aids vessel.
(b) An average of four light-twin aircraft daily along the littoral and two to four patrols per month through the contiguous AFZ by RAAF Orions.
(2) There were two reported sightings of confirmed illegal activity by foreign fishing vessels in the north-west during 1979-80. Each was of a pair of Taiwanese trawlers operating inside the then Declared Fishing Zone (DFZ). Both pairs were subsequently boarded outside the DFZ and the masters were severly reprimanded.
From time to time during the year, Indonesian subsistence fishing vessels were found to be engaged in activities not in accordance with the bilateral Memorandum of Understanding between the Australian and Indonesian Governments. Action was initiated through diplomatic channels. A higher rate of deterrent patrols was mounted and ultimately enforcement action including prosecution of the masters of two vessels ensued.
(3) See (2).
Damage to Navigational Installations
(Question No. 1682)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
(l) What navigational installations located on or off the north-west coast of Australia were damaged by causes other than natural causes during 1979-80.
(2) What has been the repair costs of any damage.
(3) Have any cases of damage been attributed to foreign fishermen; if so, what are the details.
Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) The following marine navigational installations located on or off the north-west coast of Australia were damaged by causes other than natural causes during calendar years 1979 and 1980:
(a) Dampier Decca Navigation facility-a monitor station located at Woodbrook was damaged in July 1979 when a high powered bullet holed the diesel shed wall.
(b) Port Hedland buoys-a buoy suffered minor structural damage in August 1980 when run down by a vessel.
(c) Quobba Point Lightstation-the wind vane was stoned by vandals in August 1980 causing the vane to bend.
(d) Port Hedland Decca Navigational facility-the padlocks securing the main compound, disesel shed and battery room at the De Grey station were cut with bolt cutters.
(2) The costs incurred in repairing damage to each of these facilities was as follows:
(a) Dampier Decca Navigational facility-$20.
(b) Port Hedland buoy-$I,196.
(c) Quobba Point lightstation-Nil.
(d) Port Hedland Decca Navigational facility-$30.

Crashes of Light Aircraft
(Question No. 1685)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
(1) How many crashes of (a) private and (b) commercial light aircraft occurred during the last 3 years.
(2) What type of aircraft was involved in each crash and what was the (a) date, (b) place, (c) cause and (d) number of persons killed or injured in each crash.
(3) What action is being taken to reduce the number of crashes of light aircraft.
Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) During the years 1978 to 1980 inclusive 74S accidents involving general aviation aircraft were reported to my Department. These accidents can be grouped by type of operation as follows:
(a) private-333 (b) charter-64 (c) commuter-IS (d) agricultural-87 (e) flying training-I 02 (f) other aerial work-145
(2) The detailed information requested by the honourable member in respect of each of the aforementioned accidents, whilst generally available within the data recording system, is not in a form which readily lends itself to answering the honourable member's question. It would be very timeconsuming to compile the specific answers in the form requested and I consider that the diversion of available resources to this task is not warranted without further justification. If the honourable member has in mind some particular aspects of light aircraft safety which he wishes to pursue I would be very happy to look into the matter of providing relevant statistical data more readily obtainable from Departmental records.
(3) An investigation is carried out by the Air Safety Investigation Branch of my Department into all reported aircraft accidents and air safety incidents. Relevant findings arising from these investigations are conveyed to the appropriate regulatory Branches of my Department so that any necessary corrective action can be taken as part of a continuing safety monitoring process which addresses itself to all aspects of aircraft operation.
Golden Shouldered Parrots
(Question No. 1766)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for the Capital Territory, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
What conditions obtain in the Australian Capital Territory concerning the keeping, bringing into the Territory and sale, both within and without the Territory, of Golden Shouldered Parrots.
Mr HodgmanThe answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
I am informed by my Department that the Animal and Birds Protection Ordinance 1918 provides protection for most native bird species, including the Golden Shouldered Parrot, in the Australian Capital Territory. The Golden Shouldered Parrot does not occur in the wild in south-eastern Australia and any specimens which are kept must have been bred in

captivity or imported from interstate. A permit is required to keep native birds, but there is no restriction on the sale of legally held birds. The present Ordinance does not specify the need for permits covering the import into or export of birds from the Australian Capital Territory. but the recently made Nature Conservation Ordinance will rectify this omission. The legislation applying in each of the States (except Victoria) and the Northern Territory prohibits the export of native birds without a permit and requires an import permit to be issued by the receiving State or Territory. The Australian Capital Territory liaises with State and Territory authorities to establish that their legislative requirements have been met before issuing import permits for native birds to be brought into the Australian Capital Territory. In addition, all States and Territories (including the Australian Capital Territory) are parties to an administrative agreement reached by the Council of Nature Conservation Ministers whereby the interstate movement of certain endangered species (which includes Golden Shouldered Parrots) is restricted.

Community Development Fund. The grant comprised a $6,000 general grant to assist the Society with administrative overheads and in the operation of Blundell's Farmhouse together with an amount of $1,450 for capital and equipment purposes.

Premiums on Leases to Churches
(Question No. 1801)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for the Capital Territory, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
Were the premiums on leases granted to the Church of England at Evatt, $2,400, and at Wanniassa, $5,100, to the Roman Catholic Church at Waramanga, $11,200 and to the Salvation Army at Wanniassa, $12,500; if so, how were the premiums determined.
Mr Hodgman-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
T am informed by my Department that the premiums charged were as stated. Except in the case of the Salvation Army site at Wanniassa, the premiums were determined having regard to the policy generally applying to leases granted for church purposes. This policy provides for the premium to be one third of the current assessed cost per hectare of providing serviced land.
The two Church of England sites were for residences integrated with church developments and the lower prices reflect the smaller block sizes. The Roman Catholic and Salvation Army sites were significantly larger, being for a church and a churches centre respectively.
The standard pricing policy suggested a premium charge of $21,600 for the Salvation Army site but because the site required considerable fill, the premium was reduced to $12,500 on valuation advice.

Rabbit Destruction Ordinance
(Question No. 1803)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for the Capital Territory, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
(1) What animals have been declared noxious under the Rabbit Destruction Ordinance, and what are the gazetted scalp bonuses for each.
(2) When were the animals declared noxious and when were the bonuses last reviewed.
Mr Hodgman-The answers to the honourable member's questions are as follows:
(1) Native dogs, foxes, hares, crows and cormorants have been declared noxious and that no scalp bonus is currently paid.
(2) Native dogs, foxes, hares, crows and cormorants were declared noxious most recently on 3.1.57. Hares previously had been declared noxious and bonuses were paid during nine periods from 1937 to 1953 at the rate of 3d per scalp. Since 1953 no bonus has been declared or paid for any animal. There is no intention of re-introducing scalp bonuses.

Aboriginal Sacred Sites in Western Australia
(Question No. 1844)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister representing the Minister for Aboriginal Affairs, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
Why were no sacred sites in Western Australia included in the purchase proposals drawn up by the Aboriginal Land Fund Commission following the Land Need Conference of August 1978.
Mr Viner-The Minister for Aboriginal Affairs has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
The list compiled by the Aboriginal Land Fund Commission reflected the priorities expressed by participants in the Conference. A number of Western Australian properties in the list were included, at least in part, because they contained sites of traditional religious significance to local Aboriginals.
Endangered Species of Australian Birds
(Question No. 1856)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Home Affairs and Environment, upon notice, on 9 April 1981 :
What action has the Government taken to (a) preserve endangered species of Australian birds and (b) reduce the risks to indigenous wildlife posed by feral cats.
Mr Wilson-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
Nature conservation in t!le States is the responsibility of State Governments, so actions to protect endangered species such as birds would be carried out by State wildlife authorities. The Commonwealth Government is aware of its responsibility to ensure protection of species in areas under its control, particularly the External Territories. In consultation with other Government Departments and local authorities, the Australian National Parks and Wildlife Service examines possible threats to endangered species of birds in the Territories and studies or surveys may be carried out to ensure protection of those species. Examples of such actions are the recent study of Abbott's Booby on Christmas Island and the survey of the Green Parrot on Norfolk Island.
Australia is a Party to the Convention on International Trade in Endangered Species of Wild Fauna and Flora, which gives protection to endangered species of birds likely to be affected adversely by trade. Additionally, the Agreement between the Government of Australia and the Government of Japan for the Protection of Migratory Birds and Birds in Danger of Extinction and Their Environment has been ratified, thereby affording extra protection to endangered species of birds and their habitats.
All wildlife authorities have expressed concern about the threats to native fauna from increasing numbers of feral cats. This prompted the Australian National Parks and Wildlife Service to support a consultancy project, 'Ecological Studies of Feral Cats in South-eastern Australia'. These studies were carried out during 1977-1980 by officers of the Victorian Vermin and Noxious Weeds Destruction Board, and their findings are now being appraised.

Telephone Calls by Staff: Department of Administrative Services
(Question No. 1886)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Administrative Services, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
(1) Has his attention been drawn to a report in the West Australian of 18 July 1979, page 37, that London metropolitan police reduced its telephone bill by $985,000, or about 20% by use of a monitoring device which recorded length, time and destination of telephone calls and the extension from which the call was made.
(2) Are personal telephone calls, particularly trunk and STD calls, made by staff of his Department controlled; if so, in what way.
(3) Has any consideration been given to the introduction of monitoring devices for telephone calls in his Department; if so, what was the outcome of the consideration.
Mr Newman-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) Yes.
(2) Except in the case of some senior officers or where there is a demonstrable need, all Departmental telephones have been barred for STD calls. Officers have been advised that Departmental telephones are provided only for official purposes and have been instructed to avoid making private calls unless they are urgent and unavoidable. Private trunk calls are permitted to be made only with specific approval, and must be paid for. Official trunk calls cannot be made without authorisation by a senior officer. No Departmental telephones are provided with an ISD (International Subscriber Dialling) facility.
(3) Yes. Prior to the introduction of STD bars on telephones the Department had installed PETRA call recording devices. It is not considered economic to use these devices to monitor local calls.

Community Development Employment Project
(Question No. 1906)
Mr Campbell asked the Minister representing the Minister for Aboriginal Affairs, upon notice, on 9April1981:
(I) Is the Minister satisfied with the operation of the cur rent Community Development Employment Project.
(2) Is it a fact that this scheme was designed to create jobs for unemployed Aboriginal people in their remote communities.
(3) If so, is the scheme therefore removing from the De partment of Social Security the need for payments of unemployment benefits to individuals in the communities in which the CDEP is operating.
(4) Has there been a consequent reallocation of funds from the Department of Social Security to the Department of Ab original Affairs to cover the cost of operating this scheme.
(5) Is the Minister able to say whether those communities
which have opted to utilise the scheme are now finding that the Department of Aboriginal Affairs is unable to provide enough funds for the employment of all those seeking work.
(6) If so, are some Aboriginal communities now in receipt
of significantly reduced overall incomes.
(7) Does the scheme at present make adequate provision to ensure that in those remote communities in which the CDEP now operates all those Aboriginal men and women who are seeking work are able to obtain paid employment.
(8) Is CDEP at present under review; if so, will the Minister endeavour to obtain for the Department of Aboriginal Affairs a reallocation from Treasury of the funds saved by the Department of Social Security since the introduction of this scheme.
(9) Does the Minister consider the CDEP to be a worthwhile scheme enabling Aboriginal people in their own com munities to utilise the income available to them for their own Council controlled, self-managed development; if so, will the Minister ensure that this worthwhile project is not allowed to wither because of any failure of respective Departments to or ganise the necessary budget reallocations.
( 10)Will the Minister ensure that the scheme is adequately funded so as not to be a drain on the other funds of the De partment of Aboriginal Affairs.
(11)Will the Minister also ensure that the scheme is funded sufficiently to ensure that jobs are available to all those Aboriginal people in these remote communities, both men and women, who are seeking work.
(12) In what locations has the CDEP been introduced in Western Australia.
(13) Is the Minister considering introducing the scheme into any other communities in Western Australia; if so, which ones.
Mr VinerThe Minister for Aboriginal Affairs has provided the following answer to the honour able member's question:

(1) Since its inception in 1977 the Community Development Employment Project (CDEP) Scheme has been introduced to 18 communities throughout Australia on a pilot basis. In keeping with the pilot status of the Scheme, a review was commenced in 1980. This revealed some unsatisfactory features of the Scheme and modifications to it are under consideration.
(2), (3) and (4) The CDEP Scheme was introduced in di rect response to Aboriginal communities seeking an alterna tive to unemployment benefits. Due to the application of the work test, Aboriginals engaged in work as part of a CDEP Scheme are not normally eligible for unemployment benefit. All the costs of the Scheme are met by Department of Aboriginal Affairs.
(5), (6) and (7) The Scheme made available to communi
ties new funds for wages and the cost of equipment and material associated with community projects, and it is un likely that its introduction resulted in a reduction of overall incomes. Some communities have approached me about increasing the numbers of people in'their CDEP workforce and, consequently, their CDEP funds. Given other priority demands on my Department's funds, I have been unable to agree to these requests. People who are unable to get work in CDEP communities because of restricted CDEP funds are en titled to apply for unemployment benefits.
(8) and (9) Decisions taken on the future of the Scheme, arising out of the review mentioned above, will include de cisions on the appropriate source of funds. I consider that the CDEP Scheme has many worthwhile features, such as self management and community development mentioned by the honourable member. Any changes arising from the review of the CDEP Scheme would retain the good features of the present Scheme.
(10) and (II) Funds for CDEP Schemes in particular com munities can only be applied at the expense of other projects administered by my Department. Therefore the allocation of funds to CDEP Schemes needs to be seen in the light of other competing priorities.
(12) Wiluna, Oombulgurri, Warburton, Jameson, Black stone,Wingellina andWarakurna.
(13) and (14) A number of communities in the Torres Strait, Central Australia andWestern Australia have asked to be included in the CDEP Scheme Expansion of the Scheme has been suspended, however, pending the outcome of the re view referred to above.

2,4-D
(Question No. 788)
Mr Howe asked the Minister for Primary Industry, upon notice, on 11 March 1981:
(I) Is he able to say whether the National Parks Service in the United States of America has banned the use of the chemicaI2,4-D; if so, is he able to say what were the reasons for this ban.
(2) What quantities of this chemical were (a) imported into and (b) manufactured in Australia in each of the last 5 years.
(3) Which companies manufactured 2,4-D in (a) the United States of America and (b) Australia.
(4) What evaluations from the point of view of potential impact on human health have been carried out of this toxic chemical in Australia.
(5) Are there currently any restrictions on the use of 2,4-D in any Australian State or Territory.
Mr Nixon-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) I am advised that the policy of the United States Department of Interior (USDI) is to maintain national parks in as natural a state as possible and that, wherever practicable, attempts are made to correct problems using natural and biological means before resorting to use of chemicals. The USDI

accepts compounds approved by the US. Environmental Protection Agency and does not specifically exclude additional compounds. However, when it is intended that a chemical programme be carried out in a national park, full details must be submitted to the USDI for approval. Approval is based on the type of problem and the relative cost efficiency of natural controls as compared to chemical treatment.
I am further advised that reports from the USA. that the USDI was to halt use of 2,4-D in national parks may have resulted from a misconstrual of the policy outlined above.
(2) (a) Imports of 2,4-dichlorophenoxyacetic acid and its salts and eslers, which I am advised constitute the technical grade material used in the formulation of herbicides containing 2,4-D, are classified under Item 29.16.200 of the Australian Customs Taritf.
