(4) 2,4-D herbicides have been widely used in Australia for over thirty years. Over that time continuing review has been maintained by Australian authorities of new scientific data generated world-wide on their properties, effect and fate. in addition, Australian authorities have had access to evalu ations by international bodies such as the Food and Agricul ture Organisation of the United Nations and the World Health Organisation.
(5) A precondition for the sale of 2,4-D herbicides, like other agricultural chemicals, in Australian States is that they be registered with appropriate State authorities, with claims for use, directions, and other label statements being subject to control in this respect. 2,4-D-based herbicides are approved only for uses and in the manner detailed in registered product labels.

Aid Projects in Western Samoa (Question No. 841)
Mr Jull asked the Minister for Foreign Affairs, upon notice, on 24 March 1981:
(1) Are aid projects being sponsored by the Commonwealth Government in Western Samoa in 1980-81; if so, what are the details?
(2) How many Australian personnel are being employed on each project?
(3) How many Western Samoan nationals are employed on each project?
(4) Who are the Australian personnel employed on a per manent or contractual basis on each project and what is their (a) designation and (b) salary?

(5) Are any Australians employed on a casual basis on these projects; if so, (a) who are they, (b) what is their clas sification and (c) what pay rates are applicable to them?
(6) Is overtime payable to either contractual or casual staff on these projects; if so, what accounting arrangements are made to ensure that overtime claims are legitimate?
Mr Street-The answer to the honourable
member's question is as follows:
(1) Aid projects are being sponsored by the Commonwealth Government in Western Samoa as a major part of the Australian aid program to that country. Of the total budget al location for Western Samoa in 1980-81 of $4.19 million: $3,200,000 is budgeted for project aid, $200,000 for Staffing Assistance, $250,000 forFood Aid, $140,000 for Training and $400,000 for the Development Import Grant.
(2) There is one Australian presently employed by the Australian Government, as a Works Supervisor supervising re pairs to Apia Wharf. Other Australians are employed under the Australian Staffing Assistance Scheme and the Development Imports Grant Scheme. Under both these Schemes, the employees are engaged by and are responsible to the Western Samoa Government.
(3) The Australian Government is employing no Western Samoa nationals on aid projects. Where Western Samoa nationals are working on aid projects for which some funds have been provided by the Australian Government, they are employed by the Western Samoa Government either with payment from funds provided by the Western Samoa Government or under grants from Australia which are accouilted for by the Western Samoa Government.
(4) The Australian Works Supervisor mentioned in (2) above is Mr A. Watt from the Department of Housing and Construction. His salary is $17,514 p.a. There are at present 9 Australians employed under the Australian Staffing Assist ance Scheme by the Western Samoa Government. The salary supplementation budgeted by Australia is an average of $20,000 per position. This includes provision for return air fares, education allowances for children, cost of living allow ance and inducement allowance. As at January 1981, their names and designations are:
D. Pedrana-Chief Architect K. Newton-Cocoa Agronomist C. Pedrana-ChiefFood Technologist F. L!oyd-Chieflnspector, Inland Revenue B.Fisher-Assistant Manager, Development Bank J. Moroney-Commissioner of Inland Revenue K. Tong-Water Design Engineer J. Worral-General Man a g e r , E l e c t r i c Power Corporation Dr Pittar-Pathologist
Australians employed under the Development Import Grant Scheme are employed under consultancy contracts as follows:
(a) Banana Quality Control Contract Mr T. Eady
(b) Faleolo Airport Upgrading Study Sir Alexander Gibb
& Partners
(5) No Australians are employed in Western Samoa di rectly by the Australian Government on a casual basis. Local labour, including casual labour employed on aid projects for which funds have been provided by the Australian Government, is the responsibility of the Western Samoa Government, which provides to the Australian Government statements of expenditure, accounting by category, and certified by the Financial Secretary of Western Samoa.
(6) Any overtime payments made to personnel who may be employed directly by Australia are verified according to Australian Government procedures. Payments made to personnel

Apprentices: Commonwealth Departmental Staff Ceilings
(Question No. 844)
Mr Humphreys asked the Minister for Industry and Commerce, upon notice, on 24 March 1981:
(I) In his capacity as Chairman of the Committee of Review of Commonwealth Functions reviewing Commonwealth departmental staff ceilings, has account been taken of the recommendation of the Departments of Labour Advisory Committee (DOLAC) Working Party regarding the desirability of dissociating Government-employed apprentices from staff cutback and policies of attrition; if so, what.
(2) Will Government employed and trained apprentices be excluded from these policies.
Sir Phillip Lynch-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) and (2) Consideration by the Government of the recommendations of the DOLAC Working Party took place before the Committee on Review of Commonwealth Functions was established.
A decision was taken by the Government during the latter half of 1980 which has changed the method of counting apprentices for staff ceilings purposes.
Under the new arrangements separate ceilings are determin.ed for apprentices and the apprentice ceiling cannot be used for other types of staff.
The Government has directed departments and authorities to recruit enough apprentices to ensure that they are selfsufficient in relation to apprentice training; that is, they recruit as many apprentices as are requited to maintain expected levels of tradesmen. This direction gave effect to the relevant recommendation of the DOLAC Working Party.
The total apprentice intake approved by the Government for 1981, in the Commonwealth area suubject to ceilings, including apprentices trained under the Group One Year Apprentice Scheme, is the highest level recorded in anyone year for the Commonwealth Service.

Cost of Public Relations Campaigns
(Question No. 870)
Mr Humphreys asked the Minister for Home Affairs and Environment, upon notice, on 25 March 1981:
What is the cost of public relations campaigns associated with the (a) Life. Be in it. and (b) National Day campaigns.
Mr Wilson-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(a) The Commonwealth appropriated $700,000 in 1980-81 for the national Life. Be in it program. These funds are used to promote the program nationally and are expended mainly on a national advertising campaign and the production of promotional and resource material to reinforce the advertising messages.
None of these funds are used to engage Public Relations Organisations to conduct public relations campaigns for Life. Be in it.
(b) The arrangements for celebrating Australia Day are handled through the National Australia Day Committee under the chairmanship of Mr Herb Elliott, M.B.E.
The Committee prepared a co-ordinated campaign for Australia Day 1981 with the assistance of a Public Relations consultant. The cost of such assistance was $45,000.

Cocoa Research Project at Nu'u, Western Samoa
(Question No. 1640)
Mr Dungey asked the Minister for Foreign Affairs, upon notice, on 9 April1981:
(I) What progress has been made on the cocoa research project at Nu'u, Western Samoa?
(2) What sum has been spent by Australia on this project since commencement, and what have been the 111ajor items of expenditure?
(3) Where larger purchases of materials, buildings, plant and fittings have had to be imported into Western Samoa, have they come from Australian suppliers; if not, from where have they come and why have they come from that source?
(4) What changes of Australian staff have taken place over the last 2 years?
(5) What are the names of relatives of the Australian project manager, Mr K. Newton, or his wife, who have been engaged on the project either on a wages or contract basis and what sum has been paid to each?
Mr Street-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:

(I) The cocoa research station at Nu'u is a Western Samoa Government project designed by the Western Samoa Government and based on a report prepared by a cocoa expert. It is partially funded under Austraian aid: funds are provided in the form of an accountable cash grant for the establishment of the station and a staffing assistance grant supplementing local salaries to enable the Western Samoa Government to employ qualified Australians to staff the station.
Establishment of the Australian funded components of the infrastructure for the station is nearly completed. This includes clearing of land and preparation of the site, supply and erection of four prefabricated houses, prefabricated officejlaboratory, nurseryjpropagator building and machinery shedjworkshop, planting of 13 acres of shade trees, importation of new types of breeding seed, propagation and planting out of new plants for seed production and the establishment of shade and fertilizer trials at various sites outside the station.
(2) Under the accountable cash grant, $510,000 has been spent on the establishment of the station and an amount of $45,000 through the staffing assistance grant.
Major items of expenditure of Australian funds have been four kit homes, prefabricated office and laboratory, two vehicles, propagator, processing unit and local costs associated with site works and erection of buildings at the station.
(3) Under the terms and conditions for the administration of accountable cash grants, any goods or supplies not locally produced, must be purchased in Australia. An amount of $Al3,170 for furniture purchased in New Zealand was not reimbursed from Australian aid funds.
(4) Two Australians were employed by the Western Samoa Government under the Staffing Assistance Scheme to fill the positions of cocoa agronomist who is in charge of the station and a cocoa development officer at the research station and they took up their positions in November 1978 and February 1979 respectively. The cocoa development officer resigned his position in September 1980 and a replacement is currently being recruited by the Western Samoa Government.
(5) As the Western Samoa Government is responsible for the management and operation of the cocoa research station, employment of local, contract or casual labour to work on the station is their responsibility.
Details have been sought from the Western Samoa Government and will be provided in a supplementary answer.

Department of Aboriginal Affairs: Employees
(Question No. 1922)
Mr Beazley asked the Minister representing the Minister for Aboriginal AfTairs, upon notice, on28 April 1981:
(1) How many employees of the Department of Aboriginal Affairs in each State have received notices informing them that their positions are to be transferred to the Aboriginal Development Commission.
(2) Is it a fact that persons opting for this transfer will lose normal public service tenure and will be employed on a 12 month contract basis.
(3) Is it also a fact that those persons not opting for the transfer will be given 6 months to find alternative employment within the public service and if they have failed to do so within a 6 month period they will then be placed on the unattached list and made redundant.
Mr Viner-The Minister for Aboriginal Affairs has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(I) ACT-8, New South Wales 12, Victoria-I, Queensland-17, South Australia--I, Northern Territory-19, Western Australia---4.
(2) No. Officers will be on loan to the Aboriginal Development Commission for a minimum period of 12 months from I July 1981. They will retain their substantive positions in the Department of Aboriginal Affairs and will retain superannuation, sick leave and long service leave rights and other normal entitlements, including the right to apply for promotion or transfer to other Public Service positions.
(3) No. Officers who actually occupy positions identified for transfer to the Aboriginal Development Commission but whose substantive positions are not so identified may choose to revert to their substantive positions from 1 July 1981.
Officers who are both the substantive and actual occupants of a position identified for transfer who choose not to accept the loan arrangement will become available [or redeployment to other positions under the appropriate Commonwealth Public Service conditions.
Australia-China Council: Media and Information Program
(Question No. 1923)
Dr Everingham asked the Minister for Foreign Affairs, upon notice, on 28 April 1981 :
(I) Has the Government spent $107,857.58 on a media and information program for the Australia-China Council in one year; if so, was any project aimed at exchange of political views or promotion of mutual understanding of political differences between Australia and China.
(2) Is part of the function of tbe Council to recommend to him the means of promoting greater awareness within each country of the other country and development of public information and assistance to Australian media and research on matters of direct importance to relations between the 2 countries.

(3) Have members of the Council been chosen Or sum~es tions Of directions to the Council determined that the most important relationship between the countries, as expressed in ministerial statements, namely strategic relationships., should not be noticed by the Council.
(4) Will the Government set up a similar Council to clarify relationships between Australia and the Union of Soviet Socialist Republics.
(5) Has the Government spent amounts comparable to that referred to in part (1) to encourage cxchange of mass media coverage of opposing views in Australia and the USSR.; if not, why not.
Mr Street-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) As recorded in the Annual Report of the AustraliaChina Council for 1979-80, $107,857.58 was spent on the media and information program of the Council. Principal projects funded under this heading which contributed to an exchange of political views or promotion of mutual understanding of political differences between Australia and China included:
The visit to Australia by a delegation of six Chinese journalists led by the Deputy Chief Editor of the People's Daily, and a second visit by a group of flvc journalists from the proposed English-language paper 10 be published hy the People's Daily. Several major book gifts to the Beijing National Library and the Chinese Academy of Social Sciences purchased in this year have provided reference material on Australia, including Australian political and strategic thinking.
In 1980-81 these programs havc continued. At present a joint press seminar is being organised in Beijing through the Australia-China Council and the Chinesc Journalists Association, to discuss the background to reporting of each country on the media of the other.
(2) Yes.
(3) The Council has been established, as stated in the order in Council, to promote greater awareness in each country of the other and the enlargement o[ thc areas of contact and exchange between Australia and China, Thc appointment of members does not relate to any consideration of strategic issues. No suggestions or directions in relation to strategic matters have been given to the Council.
(4) No.
(5) No. Such activities are precludcd by the measures imposed by the Government against the Soviet Union following that country's invasion and occupation of Afghanistan.

Soviet Union: Mr Y. Shukhevych

(Question No. 1975)

Mr Jacobi asked the Minister for Foreign Affairs, upon notice, on 29 April 1981:

(1) What stage has been reached as a result of Australian

diplomatic represcntations to the United Soviet Socialist Re-

public Ministry of Foreign AfTairs concerning Ukrainian pris-

oner of conscience Yuri Shukhevych.

,

(2) Can he say whether Mr Shukhevych is in his 30th year of imprisonment.

(3) Is he able to say whether Mr Shukhevych's aunt, Mrs S. Strokon of Sydney, had her sponsorship of Yurias an immigrant approved in 1980 by the Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs.

(4) Will the release of Yuri Shukhevych, and his migration to Australia, contribute to success in the mutal adherence to the Helsinki Final Accords.

2506 REPRESENTATIVES 14 May 1981

Answers to Questions

Mr Street-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) The Australian Embassy in Moscow has made representations to the Soviet authorities on behalf of Mr Shukhevych. The Soviet authorities have advised the Embassy that Mr Shukhevych cannot apply to emigrate until he has completed his prison sentence.
(2) Mr Shukhevych is due to complete a ten year prison sentence in March 1982. He is then due to serve a five year period of internal exile. I understand that Mr Shukhevych served terms of imprisonment from 1948 to 1958 and from 1959 to 1969. From 1969 until his imprisonment in 1972 Mr Shukhevych was in internal exile.
(3) My colleague, the Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs, has agreed that Mr Shukhevych and his family will be permitted to emigrate to Australia if the Soviet authorities allow them to leave the Soviet Union. I understand that Mrs Strokon, who is a relation of Mr Shukhevych, is prepared to act as sponsor in this case.
(4) Yes. Australia is not a party to the Helsinki Final Accords. Nevertheless we have in this case and others expressed our abhorrence of the denial of fundamental civil and political rights as officially practised in many parts of the world. The Government has conveyed these views officially to the Soviet authorities on many occasions and has called on them to abide by the human rights principles embodied in United Nations instruments and the 1975 Helsinki Final Accords.
Aboriginal Health Care
(Question No. 1986)
Mr Campbell asked the Minister representing the Minister for Aboriginal Affairs, upon notice, on 29 April 1981:

(1) What is the Aboriginal population in Western Australia for (a) the whole State, (b) the Pilbara region, (c) the goldfields and (d) the Kimberley region.
(2) What is the total annual sum allocated by the Commonwealth Government for Aboriginal health in Western Australia.
(3) What amount of these funds goes to the Western Australian State Government.
(4) Is he able to say what sum of the allocation is used by the Western Australian Public Health Department for Aboriginal health care.
(5) Which other State bodies utilise Commonwealth Government funding for Aboriginal health care and what sums do each of these use.
Mr Viner-The Minister for Aboriginal Affairs has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(I) The Aboriginal population recorded at the 1976 Census was:
(a) 26,126 for Western Australia;
(b) 2,664 for the Pilbara Statistical Division;
(c) 1,687 for the Lefroy Statistical Sub-Division (including the Shires of Laverton, Leonora, Menzies, Boulder, Coolgardie and the Town of Kalgoorlie);
(d) 6,350 for the Kimberley Statistical Division.
(2) A total of $7,105,460 was allocated by the Department of Aboriginal Affairs for expenditure on Aboriginal health programs in Western Australia in \980-81.
(3), (4) and (5) A total of $6,464,700 was allocated in 1980-81 for expenditure by the Western Australian Government, all of which is provided to the Public Health Department.
